## Chapter: Chapter 10: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

In the realm of materials science, understanding the behavior of materials under different loading conditions is of paramount importance. One such behavior that is of particular interest is the viscoelastic behavior. Viscoelasticity is a property of materials that exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This chapter, Chapter 10: Viscoelasticity, delves into the intricacies of this fascinating property.

Viscoelasticity is a complex phenomenon that is characterized by time-dependent deformation. This means that the deformation of a material under a given load can change over time. This property is particularly important in materials that are subjected to cyclic loading, such as in the case of fatigue failure. 

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of viscoelasticity, starting with the basic definitions and concepts. We will then delve into the mathematical models that describe viscoelastic behavior, including the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models. These models are represented by differential equations, which we will solve using numerical methods.

We will also discuss the experimental techniques used to study viscoelasticity, such as creep and stress relaxation tests. These tests provide valuable insights into the behavior of viscoelastic materials under different loading conditions.

Finally, we will look at some practical applications of viscoelasticity, such as in the design of polymer-based materials and structures.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of viscoelasticity and its importance in the field of computational mechanics of materials. You will also be equipped with the knowledge and skills to apply these concepts in your own research or professional work.




#### 9.4b Advantages and Limitations of Penalty Formulation

The Penalty Formulation is a powerful tool in the field of computational mechanics of materials, particularly in the context of incompressible elasticity. However, like any other method, it has its own set of advantages and limitations.

##### Advantages of Penalty Formulation

1. **Simplicity**: The Penalty Formulation is a relatively simple method to implement. It requires minimal mathematical sophistication and can be easily incorporated into existing computational models.

2. **Flexibility**: The Penalty Formulation can be applied to a wide range of problems, making it a versatile tool in the field of computational mechanics.

3. **Convergence**: The Penalty Formulation is known for its robustness and ability to handle non-convex problems. It typically converges to a solution, although the rate of convergence may be slow.

##### Limitations of Penalty Formulation

1. **Penalty Parameter Sensitivity**: The choice of the penalty parameter $\lambda$ can significantly affect the solution of the problem. If the penalty parameter is too small, the constraints may not be satisfied, while if it is too large, the solution may be sensitive to small changes in the input data.

2. **Non-Convexity**: The Penalty Formulation is designed to handle convex problems. If the problem is non-convex, the Penalty Formulation may not converge to the global optimum.

3. **Computational Cost**: The Penalty Formulation can be computationally intensive, particularly for large-scale problems. The need to solve a series of unconstrained problems can increase the computational cost.

In conclusion, while the Penalty Formulation offers a powerful and flexible approach to solving constrained optimization problems, it is important to be aware of its limitations and to use it appropriately. The choice of the penalty parameter and the nature of the problem are key factors that can influence the effectiveness of the Penalty Formulation.

#### 9.4c Applications and Examples

The Penalty Formulation has been widely used in various fields of computational mechanics, particularly in the context of incompressible elasticity. This section will provide some examples of how the Penalty Formulation can be applied in practice.

##### Example 1: Finite Element Analysis

In the field of Finite Element Analysis (FEA), the Penalty Formulation is often used to solve problems involving incompressible elasticity. For instance, consider a two-dimensional incompressible elastic plate under a distributed load. The problem can be formulated as follows:

$$
\begin{align*}
\min_{\mathbf{u}} & \int_{\Omega} \frac{1}{2} \mathbf{u} \cdot \mathbf{u} + \lambda \int_{\Omega} (\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{0}) \cdot (\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{0}) \\
\text{subject to} & \nabla \cdot \mathbf{u} = 0 \quad \text{in } \Omega, \\
& \mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0} \quad \text{on } \partial \Omega.
\end{align*}
$$

Here, $\mathbf{u}$ is the displacement field, $\lambda$ is the penalty parameter, and $\Omega$ is the domain of the plate. The first term represents the elastic energy, while the second term represents the penalty for violating the incompressibility constraint.

##### Example 2: Image Compression

The Penalty Formulation can also be applied in the field of image compression. Consider an image compression problem where the goal is to minimize the number of bits required to represent an image while ensuring that the image is visually similar to the original. The problem can be formulated as follows:

$$
\begin{align*}
\min_{\mathbf{x}} & \sum_{i=1}^{n} \mathbf{x}_i^2 + \lambda \sum_{i=1}^{n} (\mathbf{x}_i - \mathbf{0})^2 \\
\text{subject to} & \sum_{i=1}^{n} \mathbf{x}_i = \mathbf{y}, \\
& \mathbf{x}_i \in \{0, 1\}, \quad i = 1, \ldots, n,
\end{align*}
$$

where $\mathbf{x}$ is the vector of pixel values, $\mathbf{y}$ is the vector of desired pixel values, and $n$ is the number of pixels in the image. The first term represents the reconstruction error, while the second term represents the penalty for violating the pixel value constraint.

These examples illustrate the versatility of the Penalty Formulation and its potential applications in various fields of computational mechanics. However, as discussed in the previous section, the choice of the penalty parameter $\lambda$ can significantly affect the solution of the problem. Therefore, it is important to choose the penalty parameter carefully to ensure that the constraints are satisfied while minimizing the objective function.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Convergence and Accuracy

### Introduction

In the realm of computational mechanics of materials, the concepts of convergence and accuracy are of paramount importance. This chapter, "Convergence and Accuracy," delves into these two critical aspects, providing a comprehensive understanding of their significance and how they influence the results of computational models.

Convergence, in the context of computational mechanics, refers to the ability of a numerical method to approach a solution as the number of iterations increases. It is a fundamental concept that ensures the reliability of computational results. This chapter will explore the mathematical foundations of convergence, including the conditions under which a numerical method is said to converge.

On the other hand, accuracy is a measure of how close a numerical solution is to the true solution. It is a critical factor in determining the validity of computational results. This chapter will discuss the various factors that influence accuracy, such as the choice of numerical method, the discretization of the domain, and the boundary conditions.

Throughout this chapter, we will use the popular Markdown format for clarity and ease of understanding. All mathematical expressions and equations will be formatted using the TeX and LaTeX style syntax, rendered using the MathJax library. For example, inline math will be written as `$y_j(n)$` and equations as `$$\Delta w = ...$$`.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the concepts of convergence and accuracy, and be able to apply these concepts in their own computational mechanics of materials work.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of constraints ratio in computational mechanics of materials. We have learned that constraints ratio is a crucial factor in determining the stability and accuracy of a numerical solution. It is defined as the ratio of the number of constraints to the number of degrees of freedom in a system. A higher constraints ratio indicates a more stable and accurate solution, while a lower constraints ratio may result in instability and inaccuracy.

We have also discussed the different types of constraints that can be used in computational mechanics, such as Dirichlet, Neumann, and mixed constraints. Each type of constraint has its own advantages and limitations, and it is important to carefully consider which type of constraint is most appropriate for a given system.

Furthermore, we have explored the concept of constraint matrix and how it is used to represent the constraints in a system. The constraint matrix is a crucial component in solving constrained optimization problems, as it allows us to formulate the problem in a standard form that can be solved using existing algorithms.

Overall, understanding and properly utilizing constraints ratio is essential in obtaining accurate and stable solutions in computational mechanics of materials. It is a fundamental concept that is applicable to a wide range of problems and is crucial for any engineer or scientist working in this field.

### Introduction

These methods have been based on the principles of mechanics and have been used to predict the response of materials to external forces. However, in many practical applications, it is often necessary to make certain assumptions about the behavior of materials in order to simplify the analysis. This is where assumed strain methods come into play.

Assumed strain methods are a class of analytical techniques used to solve problems in mechanics of materials. These methods are based on the assumption that the strain in a material is known or can be assumed. This assumption allows us to bypass the need for complex numerical methods and solve problems in a more efficient and intuitive manner.

In this chapter, we will delve into the world of assumed strain methods and explore their applications in the field of computational mechanics of materials. We will begin by discussing the basic principles of assumed strain and how it can be used to solve problems in mechanics of materials. We will then move on to more advanced topics such as the Mohr's circle method and the strain energy method. These methods will be explained in detail and illustrated with examples to aid in understanding.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of assumed strain methods and their applications in the field of computational mechanics of materials. This knowledge will be valuable for students, researchers, and professionals in the field of materials science and engineering. So let us begin our journey into the world of assumed strain methods.




## Chapter 1:0: Assumed Strain Methods:




### Section: 10.1 Introduction to Assumed Strain Methods:

Assumed strain methods are a powerful tool in the field of computational mechanics of materials. These methods allow us to solve complex problems by making certain assumptions about the behavior of materials under different loading conditions. In this section, we will introduce the concept of assumed strain methods and discuss their applications in solving problems in structural mechanics.

Assumed strain methods are based on the principle of virtual work, which states that the work done by external forces on a system is equal to the internal work done by the system. This principle is used to derive the equations of equilibrium for a system. In the context of structural mechanics, the system can be a structure or a part of a structure.

The virtual work principle can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
\mbox{System external virtual work} = \mbox{System internal virtual work}
$$

The left-hand side of this equation represents the work done by external forces on the system, while the right-hand side represents the work done by the internal forces within the system.

To apply the virtual work principle to a system, we need to consider the internal and external virtual work for the system. The internal virtual work is given by:

$$
\mbox{System internal virtual work} = \sum_{e} \delta\ \mathbf{r}^T \left( \mathbf{k}^e \mathbf{r} + \mathbf{Q}^{oe} \right) = \delta\ \mathbf{r}^T \left( \sum_{e} \mathbf{k}^e \right)\mathbf{r} + \delta\ \mathbf{r}^T \sum_{e} \mathbf{Q}^{oe}
$$

where $\mathbf{r}$ is the displacement vector, $\mathbf{k}^e$ is the stiffness matrix for element $e$, and $\mathbf{Q}^{oe}$ is the vector of external forces on element $e$.

The external virtual work, on the other hand, consists of the work done by the nodal forces $\mathbf{R}$ and the work done by external forces $\mathbf{T}^e$ on the part $\mathbf{S}^e$ of the elements' edges or surfaces, and by the body forces $\mathbf{f}^e$. This can be expressed as:

$$
-\delta\ \mathbf{r}^T \sum_{e} \left(\mathbf{Q}^{te} + \mathbf{Q}^{fe}\right)
$$

where $\mathbf{Q}^{te}$ and $\mathbf{Q}^{fe}$ are additional element's matrices defined as:

$$
\mathbf{Q}^{te} = -\int_{S^e} \mathbf{N}^T \mathbf{T}^e \, dS^e
$$

and

$$
\mathbf{Q}^{fe} = -\int_{V^e} \mathbf{N}^T \mathbf{f}^e \, dV^e
$$

Numerical integration is often used for the evaluation of these matrices.

By equating the internal and external virtual work, we can derive the equations of equilibrium for the system. This is the basis of assumed strain methods, which allow us to solve complex problems by making certain assumptions about the behavior of materials under different loading conditions.

In the next section, we will discuss the different types of assumed strain methods and their applications in solving problems in structural mechanics.


## Chapter 1:0: Assumed Strain Methods:




### Section: 10.2 Euler Equations:

The Euler equations are a set of differential equations that describe the motion of a rigid body in space. They are derived from the Newton-Euler equations, which are the fundamental equations of motion for a rigid body. The Euler equations are particularly useful in the study of rotational motion, as they provide a simplified representation of the more complex Newton-Euler equations.

#### 10.2a Derivation and Applications of Euler Equations

The Euler equations can be derived from the Newton-Euler equations by making certain assumptions about the body's motion. These assumptions include:

1. The body is rigid, meaning that all points on the body move with the same velocity and acceleration.
2. The body is rotating about a fixed axis.
3. The body is not subjected to any external forces or moments.

Under these assumptions, the Newton-Euler equations simplify to the Euler equations, which can be written in the following matrix form:

$$
\left(\begin{matrix} {\mathbf F} \\ {\boldsymbol \tau}_{\rm p} \end{matrix}\right) =
\left(\begin{matrix} m {\mathbf I_3} & -m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\\
m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times} & {\mathbf I}_{\rm cm} - m[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\end{matrix}\right)
\left(\begin{matrix} \mathbf a_{\rm p} \\ {\boldsymbol \alpha} \end{matrix}\right) +
\left(\begin{matrix} m[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times}[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times} {\mathbf c} \\
</math>
where c is the location of the center of mass expressed in the body-fixed frame,
and
[\mathbf{c}]^{\times} \equiv
\left(\begin{matrix} 0 & -c_z & c_y \\ c_z & 0 & -c_x \\ -c_y & c_x & 0 \end{matrix}\right)
\qquad \qquad
[\mathbf{\boldsymbol{\omega}}]^{\times} \equiv
\left(\begin{matrix} 0 & -\omega_z & \omega_y \\ \omega_z & 0 & -\omega_x \\ -\omega_y & \omega_x & 0 \end{matrix}\right)
</math>
denote skew-symmetric cross product matrices.

The left hand side of the equation—which includes the sum of external forces, and the sum of external moments about P—describes a spatial wrench, see screw theory.

The inertial terms are contained in the "spatial inertia" matrix
</math>

while the fictitious forces are contained in the term:

</math>

When the center of mass is not coincident with the coordinate frame (that is, when c is nonzero), the translational and angular accelerations (a and α) are coupled, so that each is associated with force and torque components.

## Applications

The Euler equations are used as the basis for more complicated "multi-body" formulations (screw theory) that describe the dynamics of systems of rigid bodies connected by joints and other constraints. Multi-body problems can be solved by a variety of numerical algorithms, including the Gauss–Seidel method and the Newton–Euler equations.

The Euler equations are also used in the study of rotational motion in various fields, including robotics, aerospace engineering, and mechanical engineering. They provide a powerful tool for analyzing the dynamics of rotating bodies and predicting their future motion.

#### 10.2b Euler Equations for Rigid Bodies

The Euler equations for rigid bodies are a simplified form of the Newton-Euler equations that describe the motion of a rigid body in space. These equations are particularly useful in the study of rotational motion, as they provide a simplified representation of the more complex Newton-Euler equations.

The Euler equations for rigid bodies can be derived from the Newton-Euler equations by making certain assumptions about the body's motion. These assumptions include:

1. The body is rigid, meaning that all points on the body move with the same velocity and acceleration.
2. The body is rotating about a fixed axis.
3. The body is not subjected to any external forces or moments.

Under these assumptions, the Newton-Euler equations simplify to the Euler equations, which can be written in the following matrix form:

$$
\left(\begin{matrix} {\mathbf F} \\ {\boldsymbol \tau}_{\rm p} \end{matrix}\right) =
\left(\begin{matrix} m {\mathbf I_3} & -m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\\
m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times} & {\mathbf I}_{\rm cm} - m[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\end{matrix}\right)
\left(\begin{matrix} \mathbf a_{\rm p} \\ {\boldsymbol \alpha} \end{matrix}\right) +
\left(\begin{matrix} m[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times}[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times} {\mathbf c} \\
</math>
where c is the location of the center of mass expressed in the body-fixed frame,
and
[\mathbf{c}]^{\times} \equiv
\left(\begin{matrix} 0 & -c_z & c_y \\ c_z & 0 & -c_x \\ -c_y & c_x & 0 \end{matrix}\right)
\qquad \qquad
[\mathbf{\boldsymbol{\omega}}]^{\times} \equiv
\left(\begin{matrix} 0 & -\omega_z & \omega_y \\ \omega_z & 0 & -\omega_x \\ -\omega_y & \omega_x & 0 \end{matrix}\right)
</math>
denote skew-symmetric cross product matrices.

The left hand side of the equation—which includes the sum of external forces, and the sum of external moments about P—describes a spatial wrench, see screw theory.

The inertial terms are contained in the "spatial inertia" matrix
</math>

while the fictitious forces are contained in the term:

</math>

When the center of mass is not coincident with the coordinate frame (that is, when c is nonzero), the translational and angular accelerations (a and α) are coupled, so that each is associated with force and torque components.

## Applications

The Euler equations for rigid bodies are used in a variety of applications, including the study of rotational motion in mechanical systems, the design of robots and robot manipulators, and the analysis of the dynamics of spinning objects. They are also used in the study of the motion of celestial bodies, such as planets and satellites.

#### 10.2c Applications and Examples

The Euler equations for rigid bodies have a wide range of applications in various fields. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the use of the Euler equations.

##### Robotics

In robotics, the Euler equations are used to model the motion of robotic manipulators. These are systems of rigid bodies connected by joints that allow relative motion. The Euler equations can be used to calculate the forces and torques required to move the manipulator in a desired manner. This is particularly useful in the design and control of robotic systems.

For example, consider a simple two-link robotic arm. The first link is connected to the second link by a revolute joint, which allows relative rotation. The Euler equations can be used to calculate the forces and torques required to move the second link in a desired manner. This can be done by setting up the equations of motion and solving them using numerical methods.

##### Celestial Mechanics

In celestial mechanics, the Euler equations are used to model the motion of celestial bodies. These are systems of rigid bodies moving under the influence of gravitational forces. The Euler equations can be used to calculate the forces and torques acting on the body, and to predict its future motion.

For example, consider a satellite orbiting a planet. The Euler equations can be used to calculate the forces and torques acting on the satellite, and to predict its future orbit. This is particularly useful in the design and control of satellite systems.

##### Mechanical Systems

In mechanical systems, the Euler equations are used to model the motion of mechanical components. These can be simple systems, such as a pendulum, or complex systems, such as a car engine. The Euler equations can be used to calculate the forces and torques acting on the components, and to predict their future motion.

For example, consider a pendulum. The Euler equations can be used to calculate the forces and torques acting on the pendulum, and to predict its future motion. This can be done by setting up the equations of motion and solving them using numerical methods.

In conclusion, the Euler equations for rigid bodies are a powerful tool in the study of rotational motion. They have a wide range of applications in various fields, and can be used to model and predict the motion of a variety of systems.




### Section: 10.2 Euler Equations:

The Euler equations are a set of differential equations that describe the motion of a rigid body in space. They are derived from the Newton-Euler equations, which are the fundamental equations of motion for a rigid body. The Euler equations are particularly useful in the study of rotational motion, as they provide a simplified representation of the more complex Newton-Euler equations.

#### 10.2a Derivation and Applications of Euler Equations

The Euler equations can be derived from the Newton-Euler equations by making certain assumptions about the body's motion. These assumptions include:

1. The body is rigid, meaning that all points on the body move with the same velocity and acceleration.
2. The body is rotating about a fixed axis.
3. The body is not subjected to any external forces or moments.

Under these assumptions, the Newton-Euler equations simplify to the Euler equations, which can be written in the following matrix form:

$$
\left(\begin{matrix} {\mathbf F} \\ {\boldsymbol \tau}_{\rm p} \end{matrix}\right) =
\left(\begin{matrix} m {\mathbf I_3} & -m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\\
m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times} & {\mathbf I}_{\rm cm} - m[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\end{matrix}\right)
\left(\begin{matrix} \mathbf a_{\rm p} \\ {\boldsymbol \alpha} \end{matrix}\right) +
\left(\begin{matrix} m[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times}[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times} {\mathbf c} \\
</math>
where c is the location of the center of mass expressed in the body-fixed frame,
and
[\mathbf{c}]^{\times} \equiv
\left(\begin{matrix} 0 & -c_z & c_y \\ c_z & 0 & -c_x \\ -c_y & c_x & 0 \end{matrix}\right)
\qquad \qquad
[\mathbf{\boldsymbol{\omega}}]^{\times} \equiv
\left(\begin{matrix} 0 & -\omega_z & \omega_y \\ \omega_z & 0 & -\omega_x \\ -\omega_y & \omega_x & 0 \end{matrix}\right)
$$

These equations describe the motion of a rigid body in space, where ${\mathbf F}$ is the external force vector, ${\boldsymbol \tau}_{\rm p}$ is the external moment vector, $m$ is the mass of the body, ${\mathbf I_3}$ is the identity matrix, $[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}$ is the cross product matrix of the center of mass vector, and $[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times}$ is the cross product matrix of the angular velocity vector.

The Euler equations have many applications in the study of rotational motion. They are used to analyze the motion of satellites, spacecraft, and other rotating bodies. They are also used in the design of rotating machinery, such as engines and turbines.

#### 10.2b Euler Equations for Specific Problems

The Euler equations can be applied to a variety of specific problems in mechanics. For example, they can be used to analyze the motion of a spinning top, a rotating wheel, or a gyroscope. They can also be used to study the dynamics of a rotating spacecraft in orbit.

In these applications, the Euler equations provide a powerful tool for understanding the motion of rotating bodies. They allow us to predict the behavior of these bodies under various conditions, and to design systems that can control their motion.

#### 10.2c Applications and Examples

To illustrate the application of the Euler equations, let's consider the example of a spinning top. The top is a simple rotating body that can be modeled using the Euler equations.

The top has a mass $m$ and a center of mass located at $c$. The top is rotating about its axis with an angular velocity $\omega$. The external force acting on the top is due to gravity, and is given by $F = mg$.

Applying the Euler equations to this system, we obtain:

$$
\left(\begin{matrix} {\mathbf F} \\ {\boldsymbol \tau}_{\rm p} \end{matrix}\right) =
\left(\begin{matrix} m {\mathbf I_3} & -m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\\
m [{\mathbf c}]^{\times} & {\mathbf I}_{\rm cm} - m[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}[{\mathbf c}]^{\times}\end{matrix}\right)
\left(\begin{matrix} \mathbf a_{\rm p} \\ {\boldsymbol \alpha} \end{matrix}\right) +
\left(\begin{matrix} m[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times}[{\boldsymbol \omega}]^{\times} {\mathbf c} \\
</math>
$$

This system of equations describes the motion of the top. It allows us to predict how the top will rotate and how it will respond to external forces.

In conclusion, the Euler equations are a powerful tool in the study of rotational motion. They provide a simplified representation of the Newton-Euler equations, and can be applied to a variety of specific problems. By understanding these equations, we can gain a deeper understanding of the motion of rotating bodies, and use this knowledge to design and control these systems.




### Section: 10.3 Mean Dilatation Method:

The mean dilatation method is a computational technique used in the field of mechanics of materials to analyze the behavior of materials under different loading conditions. It is a simplified method that is particularly useful for understanding the behavior of materials under complex loading conditions.

#### 10.3a Mean Dilatation in Elasticity

In the context of elasticity, the mean dilatation method is used to analyze the volumetric strain of a material under different loading conditions. The volumetric strain is a measure of the change in volume of a material due to an applied load. It is defined as the ratio of the change in volume to the original volume of the material.

The mean dilatation method is based on the assumption that the volumetric strain is directly proportional to the mean dilatation, which is defined as the average of the normal strains in the three principal directions. This assumption is valid for many materials under small deformations.

The mean dilatation method can be used to derive the Euler equations, which describe the motion of a rigid body in space. The Euler equations are derived by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on a rigid body. The mean dilatation method simplifies this process by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered.

The mean dilatation method can also be used to derive the Euler-Bernoulli beam theory, which is used to analyze the behavior of beams under different loading conditions. The Euler-Bernoulli beam theory is derived by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on a beam. The mean dilatation method simplifies this process by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered.

The mean dilatation method is a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of materials under different loading conditions. It is particularly useful for analyzing the behavior of materials under complex loading conditions, where the use of more advanced methods may be impractical.

#### 10.3b Mean Dilatation in Plasticity

In the realm of plasticity, the mean dilatation method is used to analyze the volumetric strain of a material under different loading conditions. The volumetric strain in plasticity is a measure of the change in volume of a material due to plastic deformation. It is defined as the ratio of the change in volume to the original volume of the material.

The mean dilatation method is based on the assumption that the volumetric strain is directly proportional to the mean dilatation, which is defined as the average of the normal strains in the three principal directions. This assumption is valid for many materials under small deformations.

The mean dilatation method can be used to derive the plasticity equations, which describe the behavior of materials under plastic deformation. The plasticity equations are derived by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on a plastic body. The mean dilatation method simplifies this process by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered.

The mean dilatation method can also be used to derive the Drucker-Prager model, which is used to analyze the behavior of soils under different loading conditions. The Drucker-Prager model is derived by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on a soil body. The mean dilatation method simplifies this process by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered.

The mean dilatation method is a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of materials under different loading conditions. It is particularly useful for analyzing the behavior of materials under complex loading conditions, where the use of more advanced methods may be impractical.

#### 10.3c Mean Dilatation in Viscoelasticity

In the realm of viscoelasticity, the mean dilatation method is used to analyze the volumetric strain of a material under different loading conditions. The volumetric strain in viscoelasticity is a measure of the change in volume of a material due to viscoelastic deformation. It is defined as the ratio of the change in volume to the original volume of the material.

The mean dilatation method is based on the assumption that the volumetric strain is directly proportional to the mean dilatation, which is defined as the average of the normal strains in the three principal directions. This assumption is valid for many materials under small deformations.

The mean dilatation method can be used to derive the viscoelasticity equations, which describe the behavior of materials under viscoelastic deformation. The viscoelasticity equations are derived by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on a viscoelastic body. The mean dilatation method simplifies this process by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered.

The mean dilatation method can also be used to derive the Maxwell model, which is used to analyze the behavior of viscoelastic materials under different loading conditions. The Maxwell model is derived by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on a viscoelastic body. The mean dilatation method simplifies this process by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered.

The mean dilatation method is a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of materials under different loading conditions. It is particularly useful for analyzing the behavior of materials under complex loading conditions, where the use of more advanced methods may be impractical.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the assumed strain methods, a crucial aspect of computational mechanics of materials. We have explored the theoretical underpinnings of these methods, their applications, and the advantages they offer in the analysis of materials under different loading conditions. 

The assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to the analysis of materials, particularly under complex loading conditions. They allow us to make assumptions about the strain distribution in a material, which can greatly simplify the analysis process. However, these methods are not without their limitations. They are most effective when applied to materials under small deformations, and their accuracy can be affected by the validity of the assumptions made.

Despite these limitations, the assumed strain methods are a powerful tool in the computational mechanics of materials. They allow us to perform complex analyses that would be otherwise impractical, and provide valuable insights into the behavior of materials under different loading conditions. As we continue to develop and refine these methods, we can expect to see even greater advancements in our understanding of materials and their behavior.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating field of study that combines the principles of both viscosity and elasticity. It is a property of materials that exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This chapter, Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity, will delve into the intricacies of this field, providing a comprehensive guide to understanding and applying the principles of viscoelasticity in computational mechanics of materials.

Viscoelastic materials are ubiquitous in our daily lives, from the rubber in our tires to the plastic in our water bottles. Understanding their behavior under different conditions is crucial for engineers and scientists working with these materials. Viscoelasticity is particularly important in the field of computational mechanics of materials, where it is used to model and predict the behavior of materials under various loading conditions.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of viscoelasticity, including the concepts of strain, stress, and deformation. We will also delve into the mathematical models used to describe the behavior of viscoelastic materials, such as the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models. These models are represented using differential equations, which we will discuss in detail.

We will also explore the concept of time-temperature superposition, a powerful tool used in the analysis of viscoelastic materials. This concept allows us to predict the behavior of a material at different temperatures based on its behavior at a single temperature.

Finally, we will discuss the application of these concepts in computational mechanics of materials. We will explore how these concepts are implemented in software tools, and how they can be used to perform simulations and predictions of material behavior.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles of viscoelasticity and how they are applied in computational mechanics of materials. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practicing engineer, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and tools you need to understand and apply the principles of viscoelasticity.




#### 10.3b Mean Dilatation Method for Different Materials

The mean dilatation method is a versatile tool that can be applied to a wide range of materials. In this section, we will explore how the mean dilatation method can be used to analyze the behavior of different materials under different loading conditions.

##### Elastic Materials

For elastic materials, the mean dilatation method is particularly useful. As we have seen in the previous section, the mean dilatation method can be used to derive the Euler equations, which describe the motion of a rigid body in space. This is particularly useful for analyzing the behavior of elastic materials under different loading conditions.

For example, consider a beam made of an elastic material under a bending moment. The mean dilatation method can be used to derive the Euler-Bernoulli beam theory, which describes the behavior of the beam under the bending moment. This theory is based on the assumption that the beam is a straight, homogeneous, and isotropic material, and that the bending moment is the only applied load.

The mean dilatation method simplifies the analysis of the beam by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered. This is achieved by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on the beam, and by assuming that the beam is a rigid body. The mean dilatation method then allows us to derive the Euler-Bernoulli beam theory, which describes the behavior of the beam under the bending moment.

##### Viscoelastic Materials

For viscoelastic materials, the mean dilatation method can be used to analyze the behavior of the material under time-dependent loading conditions. Viscoelastic materials exhibit both elastic and viscous properties, and their behavior under loading conditions can be complex.

The mean dilatation method can be used to derive the equations of motion for a viscoelastic material under time-dependent loading conditions. This is achieved by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on the material, and by assuming that the material is a rigid body. The mean dilatation method then allows us to derive the equations of motion, which describe the behavior of the material under the time-dependent loading conditions.

##### Plastic Materials

For plastic materials, the mean dilatation method can be used to analyze the behavior of the material under plastic deformation. Plastic deformation occurs when the material is subjected to a load that exceeds its yield strength.

The mean dilatation method can be used to derive the equations of motion for a plastic material under plastic deformation. This is achieved by considering the equilibrium of forces and moments on the material, and by assuming that the material is a rigid body. The mean dilatation method then allows us to derive the equations of motion, which describe the behavior of the material under the plastic deformation.

In conclusion, the mean dilatation method is a powerful tool for analyzing the behavior of different materials under different loading conditions. It simplifies the analysis by reducing the number of equations that need to be considered, and it allows us to derive the equations of motion for the material under the given loading conditions.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating field of study that combines elements of both viscosity and elasticity. It is a property of materials that exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This chapter, Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity, will delve into the intricacies of this property and its implications in the field of computational mechanics of materials.

Viscoelastic materials are ubiquitous in our daily lives, from rubber bands to biological tissues. Their unique behavior under deformation, characterized by both viscous and elastic properties, makes them a subject of great interest in the field of materials science. The study of viscoelasticity is crucial in understanding and predicting the behavior of these materials under different conditions.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of viscoelasticity, including the concepts of strain, stress, and deformation. We will also delve into the mathematical models that describe the behavior of viscoelastic materials, such as the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models. These models, represented by differential equations, provide a mathematical framework for understanding the time-dependent deformation of viscoelastic materials.

We will also discuss the computational aspects of viscoelasticity, including the numerical methods used to solve the differential equations that describe viscoelastic behavior. These methods, such as the finite difference method and the finite element method, are essential tools in the field of computational mechanics of materials.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of viscoelasticity, its principles, mathematical models, and computational aspects. This knowledge will provide a solid foundation for further exploration into the fascinating world of computational mechanics of materials.




#### 10.4a Anisotropic Elasticity Equations

Anisotropic elasticity is a branch of elasticity that deals with materials whose properties vary with direction. This is in contrast to isotropic elasticity, where the properties are the same in all directions. Anisotropic elasticity is particularly important in the study of crystalline materials, where the arrangement of atoms can lead to different properties in different directions.

The equations of anisotropic elasticity are derived from the general expression for anisotropic elasticity. This expression is given by:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = C_{ijkl} \epsilon_{kl}
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor, $C_{ijkl}$ is the elasticity tensor, and $\epsilon_{kl}$ is the strain tensor. The elasticity tensor $C_{ijkl}$ is a fourth-order tensor that relates the stress and strain tensors. It is a key component in the study of anisotropic elasticity.

The elasticity tensor $C_{ijkl}$ can be decomposed into two parts: the isotropic part and the anisotropic part. The isotropic part is given by:

$$
C_{ijkl}^{iso} = \lambda \!\left( X \right) g_{ij} g_{kl} + \mu\!\left( X \right) \left(g_{ik} g_{jl} + g_{il} g_{kj} \right)
$$

where $\lambda$ and $\mu$ are scalar functions of the material coordinates $X$, and $g_{ij}$ is the metric tensor in the reference frame of the material. The anisotropic part is given by:

$$
C_{ijkl}^{aniso} = \alpha \left(a_i a_j a_k a_l + b_i b_j b_k b_l + c_i c_j c_k c_l\right)
$$

where $a_i$, $b_i$, and $c_i$ are unit vectors corresponding to the three mutually perpendicular axes of the crystal unit cell, and $\alpha$ is a scalar constant.

The equations of anisotropic elasticity can be used to analyze the behavior of anisotropic materials under different loading conditions. For example, consider a beam made of an anisotropic material under a bending moment. The equations of anisotropic elasticity can be used to derive the equations of motion for the beam, which can then be solved to determine the deformation of the beam under the bending moment.

In the next section, we will explore how the mean dilatation method can be used to analyze the behavior of anisotropic materials under different loading conditions.

#### 10.4b Anisotropic Elasticity Solutions

The solutions to the equations of anisotropic elasticity can be complex due to the involvement of the elasticity tensor $C_{ijkl}$. However, for certain special cases, the equations can be simplified and solved more easily. One such case is when the material is isotropic.

##### Isotropic Materials

For an isotropic material, the elasticity tensor $C_{ijkl}$ simplifies to:

$$
C_{ijkl} = \lambda \!\left( X \right) g_{ij} g_{kl} + \mu\!\left( X \right) \left(g_{ik} g_{jl} + g_{il} g_{kj} \right)
$$

where $\lambda$ and $\mu$ are scalar functions of the material coordinates $X$, and $g_{ij}$ is the metric tensor in the reference frame of the material. In an orthonormal Cartesian coordinate basis, there is no distinction between upper and lower indices, and the metric tensor can be replaced with the Kronecker delta:

$$
C_{ijkl} = \lambda \!\left( X \right) \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + \mu\!\left( X \right) \left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right) \quad \text{[Cartesian coordinates]}
$$

Substituting the first equation into the stress-strain relation and summing over repeated indices gives:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = \lambda \!\left( X \right) \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + \mu\!\left( X \right) \left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right)
$$

where $\mathrm{Tr}\, \mathbf{E} \equiv E^i_{\,i}$ is the trace of $\mathbf{E}$. In this form, $\mu$ and $\lambda$ can be identified with the first and second Lamé parameters. An equivalent expression is:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = K \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + \mu \left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right)
$$

where $K = \lambda + (2/3) \mu$ is the bulk modulus, and $\mathbf{\Sigma}$ is the shear tensor.

##### Cubic Crystals

The elasticity tensor of a cubic crystal has components:

$$
C_{ijkl} = \lambda g_{ij} g_{kl} + \mu \left(g_{ik} g_{jl} + g_{il} g_{kj} \right) + \alpha \left(a^i a^j a^k a^l + b^i b^j b^k b^l + c^i c^j c^k c^l\right)
$$

where $\mathbf{a}$, $\mathbf{b}$, and $\mathbf{c}$ are unit vectors corresponding to the three mutually perpendicular axes of the crystal unit cell. The coefficients $\lambda$, $\mu$, and $\alpha$ are scalars; because they are coordinate-independent, they are intrinsic material constants. Thus, a crystal with cubic symmetry is described by three independent elastic constants.

#### 10.4c Anisotropic Elasticity Examples

To further illustrate the concepts of anisotropic elasticity, let's consider some examples.

##### Example 1: Isotropic Material

Consider an isotropic material with Lamé parameters $\lambda = 100$ MPa and $\mu = 50$ MPa. If we apply a stress of 10 MPa in the $x$ direction, what is the resulting strain?

Using the stress-strain relation, we have:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = \lambda \!\left( X \right) \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + \mu\!\left( X \right) \left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right)
$$

Substituting the given values, we get:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = 100 \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + 50\left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right)
$$

Since we are applying a stress in the $x$ direction, $\sigma_{11} = 10$ MPa. Solving for the strain, we get:

$$
\epsilon_{11} = \frac{\sigma_{11}}{E} = \frac{10}{100 + 50} = 0.1
$$

##### Example 2: Cubic Crystal

Consider a cubic crystal with elastic constants $\lambda = 100$ MPa, $\mu = 50$ MPa, and $\alpha = 20$ MPa. If we apply a stress of 10 MPa in the $x$ direction, what is the resulting strain?

Using the stress-strain relation for a cubic crystal, we have:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = K \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + \mu \left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right) + \alpha \left(a^i a^j a^k a^l + b^i b^j b^k b^l + c^i c^j c^k c^l\right)
$$

Substituting the given values, we get:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = 100 \delta_{ij} \delta_{kl} + 50\left(\delta_{ik} \delta_{jl} + \delta_{il} \delta_{kj} \right) + 20\left(a^i a^j a^k a^l + b^i b^j b^k b^l + c^i c^j c^k c^l\right)
$$

Since we are applying a stress in the $x$ direction, $\sigma_{11} = 10$ MPa. Solving for the strain, we get:

$$
\epsilon_{11} = \frac{\sigma_{11}}{E} = \frac{10}{100 + 50 + 20} = 0.1
$$

These examples illustrate the application of the anisotropic elasticity equations to different materials. In the next section, we will discuss the numerical methods for solving these equations.

The chapter has provided a comprehensive guide to understanding the assumptions made in these methods and how they affect the accuracy of the results obtained.

We have also discussed the various types of assumed strain methods, including the linear and non-linear methods, and how they are used in the analysis of different types of materials. The chapter has also highlighted the importance of understanding the limitations of these methods and the need for careful selection of the appropriate method for a given material and loading condition.

In conclusion, the assumed strain methods are powerful tools in the field of computational mechanics of materials. They provide a systematic and efficient way of analyzing the behavior of materials under various loading conditions. However, it is important to remember that these methods are based on certain assumptions and simplifications, and their results should be interpreted with caution.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating field of study that combines elements of both viscosity and elasticity. It is a property exhibited by certain materials, including polymers, biological tissues, and some metals, where they exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This chapter will delve into the principles and applications of viscoelasticity, providing a comprehensive understanding of this complex phenomenon.

The study of viscoelasticity is crucial in many fields, including materials science, mechanical engineering, and biomechanics. It is particularly important in the design and analysis of structures and components that are made from viscoelastic materials. Understanding viscoelasticity can help engineers and scientists predict how these materials will behave under different loading conditions, and design structures that can withstand these behaviors.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of viscoelasticity, including the concepts of strain, stress, and deformation. We will also discuss the mathematical models that describe the behavior of viscoelastic materials, such as the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models. These models are represented by differential equations, which we will solve using numerical methods.

We will also delve into the applications of viscoelasticity, including the design of viscoelastic structures and components, and the analysis of biological tissues. We will discuss how the principles of viscoelasticity can be used to understand and predict the behavior of these materials and structures.

You should also be able to understand and predict the behavior of these materials under different loading conditions.

Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practicing engineer, we hope that this chapter will be a valuable resource for you.




#### 10.4b Anisotropic Assumed Strain Methods

Anisotropic assumed strain methods are a class of numerical techniques used in computational mechanics to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. These methods are particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary with direction, such as in crystalline materials.

The basic idea behind anisotropic assumed strain methods is to approximate the strain in the material by a set of assumed strains. These assumed strains are then used to construct a stiffness matrix, which is used to solve the equations of motion for the system.

The assumed strains are typically chosen to be simple functions of the material coordinates $X$, such as linear or quadratic functions. The choice of assumed strains can have a significant impact on the accuracy and efficiency of the method.

The stiffness matrix $C_{ijkl}$ is constructed from the assumed strains $\epsilon_{ij}$ as follows:

$$
C_{ijkl} = \frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial \epsilon_{kl}}
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor. The stiffness matrix is then used to solve the equations of motion for the system, which can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial x_j} = 0
$$

where $x_j$ is the displacement in the $j$ direction.

Anisotropic assumed strain methods are particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary with direction, such as in crystalline materials. They allow for the efficient and accurate solution of problems involving anisotropic materials, making them an essential tool in the field of computational mechanics.

In the next section, we will discuss some specific examples of anisotropic assumed strain methods, including the gradient discretisation method and the nonconforming finite element method.

#### 10.4c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical applications and examples of anisotropic assumed strain methods. These methods have been used in a variety of fields, including solid mechanics, structural analysis, and material science.

##### Example 1: Gradient Discretisation Method

The Gradient Discretisation Method (GDM) is a numerical technique that is particularly well-suited to anisotropic assumed strain methods. The GDM is based on the concept of a gradient discretisation, which is a discrete approximation of the gradient of a function.

The GDM is used to solve problems involving anisotropic materials by discretising the gradient of the strain energy function. This is done by approximating the gradient with a finite difference, which is then used to construct the stiffness matrix.

The GDM has been used in a variety of applications, including the analysis of anisotropic materials, the design of composite structures, and the study of phase transformations in materials.

##### Example 2: Nonconforming Finite Element Method

The Nonconforming Finite Element Method (NCFEM) is another numerical technique that is often used in conjunction with anisotropic assumed strain methods. The NCFEM is particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary with direction, such as in crystalline materials.

The NCFEM is used to solve problems involving anisotropic materials by discretising the material domain into a set of nonconforming finite elements. These elements are then used to approximate the strain in the material, which is used to construct the stiffness matrix.

The NCFEM has been used in a variety of applications, including the analysis of anisotropic materials, the design of composite structures, and the study of phase transformations in materials.

##### Example 3: Anisotropic Elasticity Equations

The equations of anisotropic elasticity are a set of partial differential equations that describe the deformation of an anisotropic material under applied stress. These equations are used in conjunction with anisotropic assumed strain methods to solve problems involving anisotropic materials.

The equations of anisotropic elasticity can be written in the following form:

$$
\frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial x_j} = 0
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor and $x_j$ is the displacement in the $j$ direction.

The equations of anisotropic elasticity have been used in a variety of applications, including the analysis of anisotropic materials, the design of composite structures, and the study of phase transformations in materials.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the theory behind anisotropic assumed strain methods, exploring the mathematical foundations and derivations that underpin these techniques.

### Conclusion

Assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to the analysis of materials under deformation. By making certain assumptions about the strain distribution, these methods allow us to solve complex problems of material deformation with relative ease. However, it is important to note that these methods are based on certain assumptions, and their accuracy depends on the validity of these assumptions.

In the realm of computational mechanics, assumed strain methods are particularly useful. They allow us to perform complex analyses of material deformation with relative ease, making them an indispensable tool in the field. 

In conclusion, assumed strain methods, while based on certain assumptions, provide a powerful tool for the analysis of material deformation. Their simplicity and ease of application make them a valuable addition to the toolkit of any computational mechanic.

#### 10.5a Overview of Mixed Methods

Mixed methods are a powerful tool in computational mechanics, combining the strengths of both assumed strain methods and finite element methods. They are particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary with direction, such as in crystalline materials.

The basic idea behind mixed methods is to use assumed strain methods to approximate the strain in the material, and then use finite element methods to solve the equations of motion for the system. This allows for the efficient and accurate solution of problems involving anisotropic materials.

The assumed strains are typically chosen to be simple functions of the material coordinates $X$, such as linear or quadratic functions. The choice of assumed strains can have a significant impact on the accuracy and efficiency of the method.

The stiffness matrix $C_{ijkl}$ is constructed from the assumed strains $\epsilon_{ij}$ as follows:

$$
C_{ijkl} = \frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial \epsilon_{kl}}
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor. The stiffness matrix is then used to solve the equations of motion for the system, which can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial x_j} = 0
$$

where $x_j$ is the displacement in the $j$ direction.

Mixed methods have been used in a variety of fields, including solid mechanics, fluid mechanics, and structural analysis. They have proven to be a valuable tool in the field of computational mechanics, providing a powerful and efficient means of solving complex problems involving anisotropic materials.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the theory and applications of mixed methods, exploring their strengths and limitations, and providing practical examples and case studies to illustrate their use in real-world scenarios.

#### 10.5b Techniques in Mixed Methods

In this section, we will explore some of the techniques used in mixed methods. These techniques are designed to take advantage of the strengths of both assumed strain methods and finite element methods, providing a powerful and efficient means of solving complex problems involving anisotropic materials.

##### Gradient Discretisation Method (GDM)

The Gradient Discretisation Method (GDM) is a technique used in mixed methods to approximate the strain in the material. It is based on the idea of discretising the gradient of the displacement field into a set of basis functions. The strain is then approximated by interpolating the gradient at the nodes of the finite element mesh.

The GDM is particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary with direction, such as in crystalline materials. It allows for the efficient and accurate solution of problems involving anisotropic materials.

The GDM can be formulated as follows:

$$
\sigma = C \epsilon
$$

where $\sigma$ is the stress tensor, $C$ is the stiffness matrix, and $\epsilon$ is the strain tensor. The strain tensor is approximated as:

$$
\epsilon = \nabla u
$$

where $u$ is the displacement field. The gradient of the displacement field is discretised into a set of basis functions $N_i$ as:

$$
\nabla u = \sum_i N_i \nabla u_i
$$

where $u_i$ are the nodal displacements. The stiffness matrix is then assembled as:

$$
C = \sum_i N_i \otimes N_i
$$

where $\otimes$ denotes the tensor product.

##### Non-Conforming Finite Element Method (NCFEM)

The Non-Conforming Finite Element Method (NCFEM) is another technique used in mixed methods. It is based on the idea of using non-conforming finite elements to approximate the displacement field. This allows for the efficient and accurate solution of problems involving anisotropic materials.

The NCFEM can be formulated as follows:

$$
\sigma = C \epsilon
$$

where $\sigma$ is the stress tensor, $C$ is the stiffness matrix, and $\epsilon$ is the strain tensor. The strain tensor is approximated as:

$$
\epsilon = \nabla u
$$

where $u$ is the displacement field. The displacement field is approximated using non-conforming finite elements $N_i$ as:

$$
u = \sum_i N_i u_i
$$

where $u_i$ are the nodal displacements. The stiffness matrix is then assembled as:

$$
C = \sum_i N_i \otimes N_i
$$

where $\otimes$ denotes the tensor product.

These techniques, along with the others discussed in this chapter, provide a powerful toolset for solving complex problems in computational mechanics. They allow for the efficient and accurate solution of problems involving anisotropic materials, making them an essential part of any computational mechanics toolkit.

#### 10.5c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical applications and examples of mixed methods in computational mechanics. These examples will illustrate how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be applied to solve real-world problems.

##### Example 1: Stress Analysis of a Crystalline Material

Consider a crystalline material subjected to a uniaxial tensile load. The material properties vary with direction, and the material is anisotropic. The goal is to perform a stress analysis of the material under the applied load.

The Gradient Discretisation Method (GDM) can be used to approximate the strain in the material. The strain tensor is approximated as:

$$
\epsilon = \nabla u
$$

where $u$ is the displacement field. The gradient of the displacement field is discretised into a set of basis functions $N_i$ as:

$$
\nabla u = \sum_i N_i \nabla u_i
$$

where $u_i$ are the nodal displacements. The stiffness matrix is then assembled as:

$$
C = \sum_i N_i \otimes N_i
$$

where $\otimes$ denotes the tensor product. The stress tensor is then calculated as:

$$
\sigma = C \epsilon
$$

This example illustrates how the GDM can be used to perform a stress analysis of a crystalline material with varying material properties.

##### Example 2: Displacement Analysis of a Non-Conforming Finite Element Model

Consider a non-conforming finite element model of a cantilever beam. The goal is to perform a displacement analysis of the beam under a point load at the free end.

The Non-Conforming Finite Element Method (NCFEM) can be used to approximate the displacement field. The displacement field is approximated using non-conforming finite elements $N_i$ as:

$$
u = \sum_i N_i u_i
$$

where $u_i$ are the nodal displacements. The stiffness matrix is then assembled as:

$$
C = \sum_i N_i \otimes N_i
$$

where $\otimes$ denotes the tensor product. The displacement field is then calculated as:

$$
u = C^{-1} \sigma
$$

This example illustrates how the NCFEM can be used to perform a displacement analysis of a non-conforming finite element model.

These examples demonstrate the power and versatility of mixed methods in computational mechanics. They provide a powerful toolset for solving complex problems involving anisotropic materials and non-conforming finite element models.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the realm of assumed strain methods, a crucial aspect of computational mechanics of materials. We have explored the theoretical underpinnings of these methods, their applications, and the benefits they offer in the field of material mechanics. 

Assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to solving complex problems in material mechanics. By making certain assumptions about the strain distribution, these methods allow us to solve problems that would otherwise be intractable. However, it is important to note that these methods are based on assumptions, and their accuracy depends on the validity of these assumptions.

We have also discussed the limitations of assumed strain methods. While they are powerful tools, they are not without their drawbacks. The accuracy of these methods can be affected by the quality of the assumed strain distribution, and they may not be suitable for all types of problems.

In conclusion, assumed strain methods are a valuable tool in the computational mechanics of materials. They provide a simplified approach to solving complex problems, but their accuracy depends on the validity of the assumptions made. As with any tool, it is important to understand their capabilities and limitations.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating and complex field that bridges the gap between the study of viscous and elastic materials. This chapter, Chapter 11, delves into the intricacies of viscoelasticity, providing a comprehensive guide to understanding its principles and applications in computational mechanics of materials.

Viscoelastic materials exhibit both viscous and elastic properties, making them unique and challenging to model. They are characterized by their ability to deform under stress and then slowly return to their original shape once the stress is removed. This behavior is a result of the material's microstructure, which allows for both viscous and elastic deformation.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe the behavior of viscoelastic materials. These models, often expressed in terms of differential equations, provide a mathematical framework for understanding and predicting the behavior of these materials under various conditions. We will also discuss the numerical methods used to solve these equations, such as the finite difference method and the finite element method.

#### 10.5b Mixed Formulations for Elasticity

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of mixed methods and their application in computational mechanics. In this section, we will delve deeper into the specific application of mixed methods in the field of elasticity.

The elasticity problem is to determine the deformation of a structure subject to prescribed displacements. This problem can be solved using the BDDC (Bond-Domain Decomposition Method), a popular method used in linear elasticity. The BDDC method can be best explained in terms of the deformation of an elastic structure.

The BDDC method involves the decomposition of the structure into smaller domains, or bonds, which are then solved separately. The solution of each bond is then assembled to form the solution of the entire structure. This method is particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary with direction, such as in crystalline materials.

The BDDC method can be formulated using mixed methods, which combine the strengths of both assumed strain methods and finite element methods. The assumed strains are typically chosen to be simple functions of the material coordinates $X$, such as linear or quadratic functions. The choice of assumed strains can have a significant impact on the accuracy and efficiency of the method.

The stiffness matrix $C_{ijkl}$ is constructed from the assumed strains $\epsilon_{ij}$ as follows:

$$
C_{ijkl} = \frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial \epsilon_{kl}}
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor. The stiffness matrix is then used to solve the equations of motion for the system, which can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \sigma_{ij}}{\partial x_j} = 0
$$

where $x_j$ is the displacement in the $j$ direction.

In the next section, we will explore some practical examples and case studies to illustrate the use of mixed methods in solving real-world problems.

#### 10.5c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical applications and examples of mixed methods in the field of elasticity. These examples will help to illustrate the concepts discussed in the previous sections and provide a deeper understanding of the application of mixed methods in computational mechanics.

##### Example 1: Crystalline Materials

Consider a crystalline material with varying material properties in different directions. The BDDC method, with its ability to decompose the structure into smaller domains, is particularly useful in solving problems involving such materials.

The assumed strains in this case could be chosen to be linear or quadratic functions of the material coordinates $X$. The stiffness matrix $C_{ijkl}$ is then constructed from these assumed strains, and the equations of motion are solved to determine the deformation of the structure.

##### Example 2: Structural Analysis

Mixed methods can also be used in structural analysis, where the structure is subjected to external forces. The BDDC method can be used to decompose the structure into smaller domains, and the equations of motion can be solved to determine the displacement of the structure under the applied forces.

In this case, the assumed strains could be chosen to be simple functions of the material coordinates $X$, such as linear or quadratic functions. The stiffness matrix $C_{ijkl}$ is then constructed from these assumed strains, and the equations of motion are solved to determine the deformation of the structure.

##### Example 3: Finite Element Method

The Finite Element Method (FEM) is another application of mixed methods in computational mechanics. The FEM involves dividing a continuous structure into a finite number of elements, and solving the equations of motion for each element. The solutions for each element are then assembled to form the solution for the entire structure.

In the FEM, the assumed strains are typically chosen to be simple functions of the material coordinates $X$, such as linear or quadratic functions. The stiffness matrix $C_{ijkl}$ is then constructed from these assumed strains, and the equations of motion are solved to determine the deformation of the structure.

These examples illustrate the versatility and power of mixed methods in solving a wide range of problems in the field of elasticity. In the next section, we will explore some advanced topics in mixed methods, including the use of higher-order elements and the treatment of boundary conditions.

This simplification allows for more efficient and effective computational models, which can be used to predict the behavior of materials under different conditions.

We have explored the theoretical underpinnings of these methods, their applications, and the advantages they offer in the analysis of materials under various conditions. 

Assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to the analysis of materials, particularly in cases where the material properties are known or can be assumed. This simplification allows for more efficient and effective computational models, which can be used to predict the behavior of materials under different conditions. 

However, it is important to note that these methods are based on assumptions, and as such, their accuracy depends on the validity of these assumptions. Therefore, it is crucial to understand the limitations of these methods and to use them appropriately. 

In conclusion, assumed strain methods are a powerful tool in the field of computational mechanics of materials. They provide a simplified approach to the analysis of materials, but their accuracy depends on the validity of the assumptions made. With a deep understanding of these methods, we can develop more efficient and effective computational models for the analysis of materials.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating field that combines the principles of both viscosity and elasticity. It is a property of materials that exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This chapter, "Viscoelasticity," will delve into the intricacies of this field, providing a comprehensive guide to understanding and applying the principles of viscoelasticity in computational mechanics of materials.

Viscoelastic materials are ubiquitous in our daily lives, from rubber bands and tires to biological tissues. Their unique properties make them essential in a wide range of applications. However, understanding and predicting the behavior of viscoelastic materials can be challenging due to their time-dependent response to applied forces.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of viscoelasticity, including the concepts of strain and strain rate, and how these properties are modeled in computational mechanics. We will also delve into the mathematical models used to describe the behavior of viscoelastic materials, such as the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models.

We will also discuss the role of temperature and other environmental factors in the viscoelastic behavior of materials. This is particularly important in industries such as polymer processing, where the viscoelastic properties of materials can be significantly affected by temperature changes.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles of viscoelasticity and how they are applied in computational mechanics. You will also be equipped with the knowledge to apply these principles in practical scenarios, whether it be in the design of a rubber seal or the analysis of a biological tissue.

So, let's embark on this journey into the world of viscoelasticity, where the fluid-like and solid-like properties of materials intertwine to create a fascinating field of study.




#### 10.6a Discretization Techniques in Lagrangian Formulation

The Lagrangian formulation is a powerful tool in computational mechanics, providing a systematic approach to solving complex problems. In this section, we will explore the discretization techniques used in the Lagrangian formulation, focusing on the Discretized Lagrangian (DL) method.

The DL method is a numerical technique used to solve the equations of motion in the Lagrangian formulation. It involves discretizing the Lagrangian equations into a set of algebraic equations that can be solved numerically. This method is particularly useful in problems where the equations of motion are nonlinear or where the system dynamics are complex.

The DL method begins with the discretization of the Lagrangian equations. The Lagrangian $L$ is defined as the difference between the kinetic energy $T$ and the potential energy $V$:

$$
L = T - V
$$

The equations of motion are then derived from the Lagrangian using the Euler-Lagrange equations:

$$
\frac{d}{dt} \left( \frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}_i} \right) - \frac{\partial L}{\partial q_i} = 0
$$

where $q_i$ and $\dot{q}_i$ are the generalized coordinates and velocities, respectively.

The DL method involves discretizing the equations of motion into a set of algebraic equations. This is typically done using a finite difference approximation, where the derivatives in the equations of motion are approximated using finite differences. The resulting algebraic equations can then be solved numerically using techniques such as the Newton-Raphson method.

The DL method has been applied to a wide range of problems in computational mechanics, including the analysis of mechanical systems, the dynamics of fluids, and the behavior of materials under deformation. It is a powerful tool for solving complex problems in the Lagrangian formulation.

In the next section, we will explore some practical applications of the DL method, demonstrating its versatility and power in solving real-world problems.

#### 10.6b Implementation of Discretized Lagrangian

The implementation of the Discretized Lagrangian (DL) method involves several steps. These steps are outlined below:

1. **Discretization of the Lagrangian Equations**: The first step in implementing the DL method is to discretize the Lagrangian equations. This involves approximating the derivatives in the equations of motion using finite differences. The Lagrangian $L$ is then discretized into a set of algebraic equations.

2. **Initialization**: The next step is to initialize the system. This involves setting the initial values for the generalized coordinates $q_i$ and velocities $\dot{q}_i$. The initial values for the forces $F_i$ are also set to zero.

3. **Iteration**: The system is then iterated until the equations of motion are satisfied. This is typically done using a numerical solver such as the Newton-Raphson method. The equations of motion are solved at each time step, and the values of the generalized coordinates, velocities, and forces are updated.

4. **Post-Processing**: Once the equations of motion are satisfied, the post-processing step is performed. This involves calculating the displacements, velocities, and forces at each time step. The results can then be visualized or analyzed.

The implementation of the DL method can be done in a variety of programming languages. For example, in Python, the DL method can be implemented using the following code:

```python
# Implementation of the Discretized Lagrangian method in Python

# Define the Lagrangian equations
L = T - V

# Discretize the equations of motion
dL = (partial(L, dot(q)) / partial(q)) - partial(L, q)

# Initialize the system
q = [q0]
dot(q) = [dot(q0)]
F = [0]

# Iterate until the equations of motion are satisfied
while not satisfied(dL):
    q, dot(q), F = solve(dL, q, dot(q), F)

# Post-process the results
displacements = [q - q0 for q in q]
velocities = [dot(q) for q in q]
forces = [F for F in F]
```

In this code, the Lagrangian equations are defined, and the equations of motion are discretized. The system is then initialized, and the equations of motion are solved using the Newton-Raphson method. The results are then post-processed, and the displacements, velocities, and forces are calculated.

The DL method is a powerful tool for solving complex problems in computational mechanics. Its implementation in Python allows for easy modification and extension, making it a versatile tool for research and education.

#### 10.6c Applications and Examples

The Discretized Lagrangian (DL) method is a powerful tool in computational mechanics, with a wide range of applications. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples of how the DL method can be used.

1. **Structural Analysis**: The DL method can be used to perform structural analysis on a variety of systems. For example, it can be used to analyze the deformation of a beam under load, the vibration of a mechanical system, or the stress distribution in a solid object. The DL method can handle non-linear systems, making it particularly useful for these types of applications.

2. **Fluid Dynamics**: The DL method can also be used in fluid dynamics. For instance, it can be used to simulate the flow of a fluid in a pipe, the movement of air around a building, or the behavior of a fluid in a turbine. The DL method can handle complex geometries and boundary conditions, making it a versatile tool for these types of applications.

3. **Material Science**: In material science, the DL method can be used to study the behavior of materials under different conditions. For example, it can be used to simulate the deformation of a material under load, the heat transfer in a material, or the diffusion of atoms in a material. The DL method can handle complex material properties, making it a powerful tool for these types of applications.

4. **Robotics**: In robotics, the DL method can be used to simulate the movement of robots. For example, it can be used to simulate the movement of a robot arm, the motion of a robot in a three-dimensional space, or the interaction of multiple robots in a shared space. The DL method can handle complex control systems, making it a valuable tool for these types of applications.

These are just a few examples of the many applications of the DL method. The DL method can be used to solve a wide range of problems in computational mechanics, making it a valuable tool for researchers and engineers.
